   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d65546766" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Advanced high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are inseparable from cathode materials with high specific capacities. Layered manganese-rich oxides (Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, 0.6&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<i>x</i>&nbsp;≤1) are promising cathode materials owing to their ease of intercalation and extraction of a considerable amount of sodium ions. However, lattice interactions, especially electrostatic repulsive forces and anisotropic stresses, are usually caused by deep desodiatin/sodiation process, resulting in intragranular cracks and capacity degradation in SIBs. Here, boron ions are introduced into the layered structure to build up B─O─Mn bonds. The regulated electronic structure in Na<sub>0.637</sub>B<sub>0.038</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (B-NMO) materials inhibits the deformation of MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, which finally achieves a gentle structural transition during the deep sodiation process. B-NMO electrode exhibits a high capacity (141&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>) at 1&nbsp;C with a capacity retention of 81% after 100 cycles. Therefore, anchoring boron to manganese-rich materials inhibits the detrimental structural evolution of deep sodiation and can be used to obtain excellent cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202306533-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202306533-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are attracting attention due to the dwindling reserves and increasing cost of lithium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0001_R_d65546492e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature">1</a>-<a href="#adma202306533-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0004_R_d65546492e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cathode materials of SIBs have been widely studied owing to their superior electrochemical performance and production costs. Among the various cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition metal oxides (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, TM&nbsp;=&nbsp;Ni, Fe, Co, Mn) and their solid solutions,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0005_R_d65546492e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a>-<a href="#adma202306533-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0009_R_d65546492e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Power Sources">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> such as Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Fe<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, NaNi<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and NaMnO<sub>2</sub>, have been extensively explored for feasible sodium (de)intercalation. Generally, sodium-deficient manganese-rich layered oxides (Na<sub>x</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, 0.6&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<i>x</i>&nbsp;&lt;&nbsp;1) are the primary research objects for studies because of the high natural abundance and low price of manganese resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0010_R_d65546492e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a>-<a href="#adma202306533-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0014_R_d65546492e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Physica B+C">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, Na-deficient layered cathodes cycled in the 2–4&nbsp;V voltage range exhibit insufficient capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0015_R_d65546492e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Commun.">15</a>-<a href="#adma202306533-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0017_R_d65546492e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Small Methods">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thence, a deep sodiation process for Na-deficient materials is required to improve their capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0018_R_d65546492e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0019_R_d65546492e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By lowering the discharge voltage to 1.5&nbsp;V, the reduction reaction of Mn in the layered structure can be fully utilized to obtain a higher capacity, while a large amount of sodium ions floods into the layered structure so that the number of sodium ions far exceeds its initial content. As a result, the deep sodiation process brings challenges to the layered structure. The intercalation and rearrangement of excessive sodium ions breaks initial electrostatic balance, and the continuous production of Mn (III) ions exhibits the Jahn–Teller effect. Therefore, it is necessary to explore the crystal structure changes during the deep sodiation process.</p>
         
         <p>Here, we selected the P’2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> material to investigate the deep sodium intercalation process. Normally, during the first charge/discharge process, the P’2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> experiences a series of phase transitions. For example, due to the two locations (prismatic and octahedral) of sodium ions, P’2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> generally experiences complicated Na/vacancy order/disorder changes during charge/discharge, which is consistent with the multiplatforms of the charge/discharge curves in SIBs, leading to slow Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0012_R_d65546492e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Sci.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0020_R_d65546492e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, when charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, P’2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> undergoes a phase transition from P’2 to OP4 due to the interlayer gliding driven by the electrostatic repulsion of O<sup>2−</sup> ions after deintercalation of some Na<sup>+</sup> ions. This phase transition produces internal stress and changes the crystal volume.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0021_R_d65546492e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a>-<a href="#adma202306533-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0023_R_d65546492e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Stor. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unlike the above two widely mentioned problems, the hazards of the deep sodiation process during the first discharge from ≈2 to 1.5&nbsp;V are often overlooked and have not yet been fully elucidated. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202306533-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the detailed lattice changes during the deep sodiation process, the intercalation of a large amount of Na<sup>+</sup> first combines with lattice oxygen, which decreases the electrostatic repulsion of O<sup>2−</sup> ions and leads to the shrinkage of the transition metal layer (MnO<sub>2</sub>). Subsequently, the increased electrostatic repulsion between Na<sup>+</sup> and the transition metal layers can even cause the gliding of the lattice layers.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202306533-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5223a32f-9a73-48ce-9569-bd7f2f4dc559/adma202306533-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5223a32f-9a73-48ce-9569-bd7f2f4dc559/adma202306533-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d6fe5b95-3bcb-4b80-a6c2-c8a0efce0d8f/adma202306533-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5223a32f-9a73-48ce-9569-bd7f2f4dc559/adma202306533-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202306533-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202306533" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration for the detrimental lattice changes of deep sodiation and the expected solution. b) Synchrotron HEXRD and c) PDF patterns for NMO and B-NMO cathode materials. d) SEM image and e) XPS spectra of B 1<i>s</i> for B-NMO material under different sputtering time.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Considering the significance of the deep sodiation process, stabilizing the structural evolution during this process is critical for improving the performance of sodium layered manganese-rich cathodes. In this study, we systematically studied the deep sodiation process of the P’2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> material and found that boron anchoring on the host materials greatly stabilized the deep sodiation process. Boron ions with a fixed valence state (+3) have been widely used as dopants in physics and chemistry. Owing to their light mass and small ionic radius (0.27&nbsp;Å), boron ions can be inserted into the crystal structure and coordinated with the oxygen atoms in layered structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0024_R_d65546492e756" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>−</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0026_R_d65546492e759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, both P’2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (NMO) and boron-anchored Na<sub>0.637</sub>B<sub>0.038</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (B-NMO) materials were synthesized via the sol–gel and high temperature calcination methods. Moreover, pair distribution function (PDF) analysis confirmed that the boron ions were successfully inserted into the interstices between the transition metal layers. In situ XRD and PDF analyses revealed that severe lattice layer contraction and anisotropic stress were produced after excessive sodium intercalation into P’2-type NMO, which led to its poor electrochemical performance. Moreover, in situ XRD and ex situ PDF results of P’2-type B-NMO material revealed uniform structural evolution during the deep sodiation process. P’2-type B-NMO cathode materials achieved a high reversible capacity of 141&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;C with the better capacity retention (81%) for 100 cycles in SIBs. Our study not only highlights the unfavorable structural changes in P’2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> material during the deep sodiation process but also provides an effective strategy to obtain high-capacity and long-life cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202306533-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202306533-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202306533-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202306533-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Synthesis and Structural Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the designed crystal structures of NMO and B-NMO, which have a layered structure with repeated transition metal layers and sodium ion layers stacked in the sequence of ABBA. In particular, boron ions are embedded in the interstices between the transition metal layers and form polyanions (BO<sub>3</sub>)<sup>3−</sup> or (BO<sub>4</sub>)<sup>5−</sup> with lattice oxygen in the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0024_R_d65546492e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0025_R_d65546492e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The strong B–O–Mn interaction is expected to inhibit the Jahn–Teller distortion of the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedron and promote a uniform crystal structure change during the deep sodiation process. The detailed preparation procedure of NMO and B-NMO materials is presented in the Experimental Section (Supporting Information). Unlike the traditional high-temperature quenching synthesis method used for P’2-type NMO materials, P’2-type B-NMO materials can be easily obtained after natural cooling. This simplified preparation process for B-NMO is beneficial for practical production. Elemental compositions of NMO and B-NMO were determined using inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES), and the results are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Although the molar mass ratio of manganese nitrate and boracic acid was 10:1 during the preparation of the precursor by the sol–gel method, some boron was lost in the subsequent high-temperature sintering process due to the low melting (171&nbsp;°C) and boiling (300&nbsp;°C) point of H<sub>3</sub>BO<sub>3</sub>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the synchrotron high energy X-ray diffraction (HEXRD) patterns of NMO and B-NMO materials in similar peak intensity, which are indexed as the P’2-type layered structure in the <i>Cmcm</i> space group (JCPDS No. 27-0752),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0018_R_d65546492e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0019_R_d65546492e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with distorted transition metal octahedrons. By locally zooming in on the first peak for both patterns, we observed that the (002) peak of B-NMO had shifted to the left due to the coordinated boron species causing the expansion of the lattice parameter <i>c</i>. A similar shift phenomenon was reported by Guo et&nbsp;al., who revealed that the increased electron density on oxygen in the BO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron enlarged the interspacing of the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and decreased the sodium ion diffusion energy barriers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0026_R_d65546492e840" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HEXRD Rietveld refinement results for NMO and B-NMO are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Considering the small atomic number and light weight of boron ions, it is difficult to identify boron in the crystal structure by HEXRD analysis, and thus the boron ions were excluded during the Rietveld refinement procedure. The HEXRD refinement results also show the enlarged <i>c</i>-axis and crystal volume of B-NMO material.</p>
            
            <p>Different from synchrotron HEXRD, which is commonly used to obtain information by integrating the overall structure, synchrotron X-ray PDF analysis can identify the local short-range (&lt;7&nbsp;Å) characteristics of the crystal structure by capturing information on the total scattering (Bragg and diffuse scattering) of X-rays by atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0027_R_d65546492e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Sci.">27</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0028_R_d65546492e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - NPG Asia Mater">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0001">1c</a> shows the PDF patterns for NMO and B-NMO. When boron ions were located at the tetrahedral site of the layered structure, they were directly bonded to the oxygen ions in a short distance. And the length of B─O bonds is smaller than the Mn─O bonds; thus the curves at lower <i>r</i> were enlarged as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that a faint bulge is appeared at about 1.5&nbsp;Å in the B-NMO curve, which most likely corresponds to the B─O bonds at the tetrahedron. The low X-ray scattering power of boron and oxygen ions makes the peak very inconspicuous. Moreover, no significant local structural difference is observed between the two adjacent transition metal octahedra, confirming that boron ions do not replace the octahedrally coordinated Mn in the structure. When the interatomic distance is enlarged beyond 8&nbsp;Å, two PDF patterns show incremental discrepancies, and these peaks indicate the distances of atoms between transition metal layers and alkali metal layers as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), corresponding to the long-range ordering in the crystal structure. In other words, with the continuous accumulation of weak influences of boron, the differences between NMO and B-NMO materials were finally indicated at high <i>r</i> regions, corresponding to the increased <i>c</i>-axis parameter and unit cell volume of B-NMO material in HEXRD results. PDF refinement results for NMO and B-NMO are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). Based on the refined PDF patterns, both the NMO and B-NMO materials showed distorted octahedrons with two long Mn─O bonds. Moreover, B-NMO material exhibited a wide interlayer Mn…Mn distance, further confirming that B ions occupied the tetrahedral sites between TMO<sub>2</sub> layers. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of B-NMO is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0001">1d</a>. Irregular complexes several micrometers in size heaped without any specific shape. The distributions of boron ions in the B-NMO material were detected by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and Time of Flight Secondary Ion Mass Spectrometry (TOF-SIMS). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0001">1e</a>, the peak at about 192&nbsp;eV in the XPS pattern is assigned to B 1<i>s</i> and its intensity shows no changes after etching 15&nbsp;s, which demonstrates that the content of boron ions has no obviously reduction in the bulk structure of B-NMO material. Specially, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) show the results of TOF-SIMS tests for the B-NMO material. In the positive mode, Na<sup>+</sup>, BO<sup>–</sup>, and Mn<sup>+</sup> ions were detected and their concentration almost maintained constantly with the increasing of sputtering time. Corresponding 2D mapping images of BO<sup>–</sup> and Mn<sup>+</sup> ions in both top and front projection show the evenly distribution of elements as well. One frame in the picture means the ion beam does a complete scan of the entire analysis area. Besides, the signal of BO<sup>–</sup> ions is more obviously in the negative mode and its concentration is very uniform with the increase of sputtering time. In brief, XPS and TOF-SIMS results reveal that boron ions were bonded with lattice oxygen and scattered uniformly in the B-NMO material. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) shows the SEM and EDS mapping images of NMO. Morphology size of NMO particles is ≈5&nbsp;µm (Supporting Information), which is smaller than that of B-NMO particles. In addition, Na, Mn, and O are equally distributed in the particles. Synchrotron X-ray scattering and chemical tests verified the successful introduction of boron into B-NMO material.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202306533-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202306533-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>Both the NMO and B-NMO materials were assembled into half-coin cells with sodium counter electrodes, and their electrochemical performances are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the curves of the sodium ion content in the cathode materials versus the operating potentials, which were calculated using the Faraday's law. Both charge/discharge curves exhibited the typical multi-platform features of cathode materials in SIBs. ICP-OES revealed that the sodium ion content started at 0.599 and 0.637 for the NMO and B-NMO cathode materials, respectively. After charging, sodium ions were deintercalated from the layered structure, and their remaining amounts were transferred to ≈0.155 for NMO and 0.262 for B-NMO at 4.3&nbsp;V. Subsequently, sodium ions were inserted back into the structure during the discharging process. Final Na<sup>+</sup> ion content in NMO and B-NMO was 0.930 and 0.892, respectively; thence, the anchoring boron ions did not markedly decrease the number of stored Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the layered structure. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2b</a> shows the dQ/dV curves of NMO and B-NMO electrodes cycled at 0.1&nbsp;C. These peaks in the dQ/dV curves are consistent with the multiple platforms shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2a</a>. Notably, the number of peaks in the B-NMO cathode materials was less than that in the NMO cathode, confirming that sodium ion transport in the B-NMO cathode is easier than that in the NMO cathode. These peaks concentrated between 2.5 and 3.2&nbsp;V correspond to the ordering/disordering changes of Na/vacancies during Na<sup>+</sup> ions transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0012_R_d65546492e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Sci.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0018_R_d65546492e943" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the charging process, the oxidation reaction from Mn (III) to Mn (IV) occurs continuously with the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, and the peaks at high voltages are described as unfavorable phase transition/lattice oxygen oxidation reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0029_R_d65546492e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0030_R_d65546492e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Eng. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the discharging process, an additional peak is observed at ≈2.1&nbsp;V for NMO cathode materials, whose inner physicochemical changes will be discussed later.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202306533-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1953c965-f2a1-4b3a-aaf8-b686262a4f28/adma202306533-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1953c965-f2a1-4b3a-aaf8-b686262a4f28/adma202306533-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a8e3421e-9c9c-47a6-9932-bdce7d08e576/adma202306533-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1953c965-f2a1-4b3a-aaf8-b686262a4f28/adma202306533-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202306533-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202306533" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of NMO and B-NMO cathode materials in SIBs. a) Charge–discharge curves versus Na<sup>+</sup> ions content and b) dQ/dV curves. c) Cycle performance at 1&nbsp;C (voltage window: 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V, 1&nbsp;C&nbsp;=&nbsp;160&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>). d,e) Charge–discharge curves at different cycles for NMO and B-NMO electrodes. f) Median voltages performance. g) CV curves, h) cycle performance at 1&nbsp;C (voltage window: 1.4–4.2&nbsp;V), and i) rate performance of B-NMO full cells coupled with hard carbon.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Long-term cycling performances of NMO and B-NMO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2c</a>. Both the NMO and B-NMO electrodes were first activated at 0.1&nbsp;C and then cycled at 1&nbsp;C. The capacity of the NMO cathode was 163&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup> at 1&nbsp;C, and it decreased to ≈84&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. In contrast, B-NMO cathode showed higher stability than the pristine NMO materials. The capacity retention of B-NMO was ≈81% after 100 cycles at 1&nbsp;C. Charge/discharge curves at different cycles for the NMO and B-NMO cathode materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2d,e</a>, respectively. For NMO cathode, the capacity decreased quickly and a few electrochemical platforms disappeared during cycling, whereas the B-NMO cathode materials showed a weaker capacity reduction and more distinctive platforms after 100 cycles. A comparison of their median voltage performances is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2f</a>. The median voltage decay of B-NMO is only ≈0.18% per cycle, which is better than 3.97% per cycle for the NMO cathode materials. The higher capacity and voltage retention of B-NMO facilitate the practical development and application of SIBs. An electrochemical comparison of NMO and B-NMO in different voltage windows is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). As the voltage window was adjusted to 1.5–4.0&nbsp;V, the initial capacities of NMO and B-NMO were very close (144&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup> for NMO and 135&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup> for B-NMO) at 1&nbsp;C, but B-NMO showed a more stable cycle performance. As the voltage window was enlarged to 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V, the capacity retention for NMO decreased sharply, ≈35%, whereas B-NMO maintained high capacity retention of 81% after 50 cycles. In addition, B-NMO exhibited higher capacity and better capacity retention than NMO at a higher current density of 2&nbsp;C. Overall, the B-NMO cathode performed well in all aspects of electrochemical testing. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) shows the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) curves and the corresponding calculated Na-ion diffusion coefficients for NMO and B-NMO. Diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> ions decreased sharply for NMO cathode materials at high voltages and was lower than that of B-NMO during the entire discharge process. The better sodium ion diffusion performance of B-NMO may be due to the increased distance along <i>c</i>-axis, which promotes the mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Besides, cyclic voltammetry (CV) at different scan rates and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were conducted for both NMO and B-NMO electrodes in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). As the increasing of scan rates, the peak currents for CV curves increased as well. And these CV curves can maintain their primary shape with a little peaks shift. According to the changes of peak currents, we further made the plots of peak currents (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) as a function of square root of scanning rate (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>), whose perfect linear relationship manifested the diffusion-controlled behavior of NMO and B-NMO during the charge and discharge process. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) has a positive correlation with the slop of linear fitting results as explained in Supporting Information. And the larger slope of B-NMO electrodes showed the faster charge transfer kinetics and larger Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion coefficient than that of NMO electrodes. EIS spectra of NMO and B-NMO electrodes can indicate the resistance for Na<sup>+</sup> ions transfer in different components of batteries. The charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at the medium frequency as shown as the semicircle of B-NMO (206&nbsp;Ω) is lower than NMO (560&nbsp;Ω), which proves the less electrode/electrolyte resistance in B-NMO electrodes. Hence, CV and EIS tests results also reveal the faster electrokinetic performance in B-NMO electrode. Next, B-NMO material was used to assemble full cells with presodiated hard carbon. Their electrochemical performance is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2g–i</a>. CV curve for the full cell exhibited a similar peak distribution with a slightly reduced redox potential compared to that of the B-NMO half-cell, indicating a reversible redox reaction in the full cell. In addition, the full cell delivered a specific capacity of ≈140&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> based on the mass of B-NMO at 1&nbsp;C, which was comparable to that of the half-cell. The rate performance for full cell was also outstanding and its capacity could reach up to ≈107&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 3&nbsp;C. By comparing the electrochemical performances of the pristine and B-NMO cathode materials in SIBs, the superior cycle stability of B-NMO demonstrated that inserting boron into a layered structure effectively improved the lifespan of SIBs and promoted further practical production and application of layered transition metal materials. Finally, the B-NMO material shows the considerable cycle retention and lifespan in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) by comparing with some reported layered manganese-rich oxides in SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202306533-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202306533-sec-0050-title">2.3 Charge Compensation Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To confirm the oxidation states of Mn in pristine NMO and B-NMO materials, XPS spectra of Mn 2<i>p</i> were gathered under different sputtering time. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202306533-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, NMO and B-NMO materials are mainly consist of Mn (III) and Mn (IV) ions in the layered structure. The average valence for NMO and B-NMO materials are +3.29 and +3.24 at the surface. After etching 15&nbsp;s, the oxidation states of Mn in NMO and B-NMO materials are increased to +3.59 and +3.50, corresponding to the higher Mn valences in the bulk region. Furthermore, X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) techniques, including hard XAS (hXAS) and soft XAS (sXAS), exhibit specific elemental selectivity and high chemical sensitivity. We used these techniques to study the charge compensation mechanism of B-NMO materials in SIBs. Mn K-edge X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectrum of pristine NMO material in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) shows a little higher energy shift than that of B-NMO material, which is consistent with the higher oxidation state of Mn in NMO based on XPS results. In operando normalized Mn K-edge XANES spectra were collected in transmission mode during the first charging and discharging processes, which provided qualitative information on the changes in the oxidation state and site symmetry of the detected manganese.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0028_R_d65546492e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - NPG Asia Mater">28</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0031_R_d65546492e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano-Micro Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As desodiation proceeded, Mn K-edge XANES spectra showed a continuous shift toward higher energies, with lighter red curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0003">3c</a>, proving the oxidation reaction of Mn. In addition, the pre-edge region of the Mn K-edge XANES spectra, located ≈15–20&nbsp;eV before the main K-edge crest, mainly shows the electronic transition from the 1<i>s</i> core to the empty 3<i>d</i> levels (A<sub>1</sub> for <i>e<sub>g</sub></i> and A<sub>2</sub> peak for <i>t</i><sub>2</sub><i><sub>g</sub></i> orbitals) and is also sensitive to the valence and symmetry of Mn.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0032_R_d65546492e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ChemSusChem">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Intensity of the high-energy shoulder (A<sub>2</sub>) apparently increased during charging in the zoom in part, exhibiting a diminished Jahn–Teller effect with increasing Mn valence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0033_R_d65546492e1102" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Small Methods">33</a></sup></span><sup>−</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0035_R_d65546492e1105" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Phys. Rev. B">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the XANES spectra of Mn K edge were shifted to lower energies and the intensity of shoulder A<sub>2</sub> in the pre-edge region gradually reduced throughout the sodiation process, indicating that the average valence of Mn was significantly reduced and some Jahn–Teller active Mn(III) ions were generated in the layered structure.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202306533-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6763f6ef-7042-4e6a-92d0-c5c923cd65ee/adma202306533-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6763f6ef-7042-4e6a-92d0-c5c923cd65ee/adma202306533-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/043ae040-6191-42e5-a600-2a99d02178ad/adma202306533-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6763f6ef-7042-4e6a-92d0-c5c923cd65ee/adma202306533-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202306533-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202306533" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS Mn 2<i>p</i> spectra and fitting results of a) NMO and b) B-NMO pristine materials under different sputtering time. c) In situ Mn K-edge XANES spectra of B-NMO material. d) O K-edge sXAS spectra in TFY mode for B-NMO electrodes at different voltage states.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition to the known redox reaction of Mn, we explored the redox reactions of lattice oxygen<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0036_R_d65546492e1137" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>−</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0038_R_d65546492e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in B-NMO using sXAS techniques. First, the Mn L-edge sXAS spectra were measured in total electron yield (TEY) mode, which has a probing depth of ≈10&nbsp;nm and is normally used to characterize the surfaces of materials. All Mn L-edge sXAS spectra, including the reference samples, MnO for Mn(II), LaMnO<sub>3</sub> for Mn(III), and SrMnO<sub>3</sub> for Mn(IV), and the tested electrodes in different states, are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). All spectra exhibit two separate regions at 636–644&nbsp;eV (L<sub>3</sub>) and 648–656&nbsp;eV (L<sub>2</sub>), which are caused by 2<i>p</i> core-hole spin–orbital splitting.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0039_R_d65546492e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Energy">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two spectral features (B<sub>1</sub> and B<sub>2</sub>) in L<sub>3</sub> region are assigned to Mn <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> and <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> sub-bands, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0040_R_d65546492e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Phys.: Condens. Matter">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, for pristine B-NMO, the L-edge spectrum of Mn (black) exhibits comprehensive characteristics of mixed valence states, as indicated by peaks at ≈639, 640, and 642&nbsp;eV. After charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, the decreased intensity of B<sub>1</sub> probably corresponds to the reducing of valence electrons in <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> orbitals, and the high-energy shift of B<sub>2</sub> peak also confirms the increasing oxidation state of Mn in B-NMO material. For discharged B-NMO, its spectrum in blue exhibited similar shape with its pristine spectrum, except for the disappearance of the small peak at ≈639&nbsp;eV, indicating that divalent manganese, which is easily dissolved in the electrolyte, was not generated during the discharge process, and this contributed to the long-cycle stability of the cathode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0041_R_d65546492e1191" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Stor. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the O K-edge sXAS spectra of the pristine and cycled electrodes were measured in both TEY and total fluorescence yield (TFY) modes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). TFY mode has a probing depth of ≈50&nbsp;nm, which can be used to probe the bulk information of B-NMO. These spectra can also be divided into two regions: the pre-edge peaks from 527 to 535&nbsp;eV are assigned to Mn Mn<sub>3d</sub> and O<sub>2p</sub> orbitals hybridization, and the broadened peaks from 537 to 545&nbsp;eV correspond to the hybridization between the Mn<sub>4sp</sub> and O<sub>2p</sub> orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0042_R_d65546492e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Stor. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0043_R_d65546492e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0003">3d</a> shows an enlargement of the pre-edge region for the O K-edge sXAS spectra in the TFY mode, revealing the discrepant oxygen behavior between the surfaces and the bulk. First two peaks at ≈529.5 and 532&nbsp;eV are assigned to the <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> and <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> bands, respectively, whereas the peak at 534&nbsp;eV is associated with the carbonate species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0037_R_d65546492e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Energy Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Here, the intensity area of the pre-edge peak correlated with the density of unoccupied hybrid Mn<sub>3d</sub>–O<sub>2p</sub> orbitals. Intensity of pre-edge in the TEY mode increased significantly after desodiation, proving that there were more holes in the hybrid orbital, whereas the intensity of the pre-edge at 532&nbsp;eV in the TFY mode showed only a slight increase. This suggests that different redox reactions occur on the surface and in the bulk structure of B-NMO. On the surface, both manganese and lattice oxygen participated in the redox reaction, leading to a sharp increase in the empty states in the spectra, whereas only manganese oxidation occurred in the layered bulk structure. Given the presence of complex organic and inorganic components in coin cells, oxygen reaction on the surface may result from electrolyte decomposition and the formation of cathode–electrolyte interfaces (CEIs). Fortunately, these surface oxygen reactions do not significantly damage the bulk structure of the B-NMO cathode. As a result, the solid block structure of the B-NMO cathode was stabilized by the presence of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which could be repeatedly inserted and extracted with excellent cyclic stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202306533-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202306533-sec-0060-title">2.4 Structural Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>To clarify the cause for the distinct electrochemical behaviors of the two cathode materials (NMO and B-NMO) in SIBs, in situ XRD techniques were used to track their lattice structure changes. Laboratory XRD equipment was used for the in situ experiments of the NMO electrode material. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202306533-fig-0004">4a</a>, the NMO electrode is in the P’2 phase (denoted as P’2-I) at the beginning of charging, and the (002) and (004) peaks shift continuously after the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Then two new peaks at ≈1.23 and 2.46&nbsp;Å<sup>–1</sup> are emerged at the voltage of 3.5&nbsp;V, indicating the generation of OP4 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0018_R_d65546492e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0021_R_d65546492e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0044_R_d65546492e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the electrostatic balance between the transition metal layers and sodium ion layers is broken, and the repulsion between lattice oxygen (O<sup>2−</sup>–O<sup>2−</sup>) causes expansion of the <i>c</i>-axis. The accumulation of repulsion subsequently leads to the gliding of the transition metal layers and finally forms the immediate phase (OP4) with a staggered arrangement of sodium ion prismatic and octahedral sites. The right shift of the OP4 (002) and (004) peaks indicates the contraction of <i>c</i>-axis after continuous desodiation. When the battery is charged to 4.0&nbsp;V, the disappearance of these diffraction peaks indicates that the order of the layered material is completely broken, which further complicates the structural identification of cathode materials at high voltages based on XRD analysis. During the sodiation process, the peaks of the OP4 phase first recur at ≈3.5&nbsp;V. With the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the (002) and (004) peaks of the OP4 phase shift to the left and vanish at 3&nbsp;V; thus, NMO is transferred back to the P’2-I phase. Subsequently, the P’2-I (002), (004), and (022) peaks underwent typical stepwise changes with Na/vacancy interactions,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0018_R_d65546492e1273" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is consistent with the charge/discharge and dQ/dV curves shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0002">2b</a>. As the cell is discharged to 2.2&nbsp;V, a new P’2-II phase appears and ≈0.797 Na was inserted into the NMO layered structure. The generation and definition of the P’2-II phase were colloquially explained to be related to the Jahn–Teller effect of Mn(III), which is unfavorable for cycling stability; however, the detailed structure of the P’2-II phase was not obtained. The chemical formula for the discharged NMO cathode can be obtained by calculating as Na<sub>0.930</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. To determine the phase structure, we compared the pattern of Na<sub>0.930</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> with that of reported NaMnO<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0045_R_d65546492e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Crystals">45</a>-<a href="#adma202306533-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0047_R_d65546492e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). However, given the poor quality of the in situ XRD pattern, it was difficult to retrieve its exact structure. We further explored the structure of the P’2-II phase in the following section.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202306533-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a059e387-a2d2-4d71-b235-390ae54e82b1/adma202306533-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a059e387-a2d2-4d71-b235-390ae54e82b1/adma202306533-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2d2d41e6-c4f0-4a93-947e-549d62364f70/adma202306533-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a059e387-a2d2-4d71-b235-390ae54e82b1/adma202306533-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202306533-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202306533" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ lab XRD patterns of NMO and b) in situ synchrotron HEXRD patterns of B-NMO electrode during the first charge and discharge process at 0.1&nbsp;C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The in situ synchrotron XRD patterns of B-NMO are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0004">4b</a>. The B-NMO electrode exhibits a simplified structural evolution compared to that of NMO. First, the immediate OP4 phase was produced at a higher voltage of 3.9&nbsp;V. This indicates that boron weakened the gliding of the transition metal layers and prevented the phase transition from P’2-I to OP4. In fact, the OP4 phase is deemed to decrease structural damage in comparison with the P2–O2 phase transition, and it contributes to the promotion of structural stability during long-term cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0019_R_d65546492e1324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Second, the B-NMO material maintains its original P’2-I structure after deep sodiation, which successfully accommodates ≈0.892 Na. The counterplot of the (002) peak shows a reduced intensity without any new peak emergence. The position of the (002) peak shifted to the right at the end of the discharge, which corresponded to the <i>c</i>-axis contraction after the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. To sum up, the B-NMO electrode experiences the reversible phase transition from P’2-I→OP4→P’2-I, while the NMO electrode goes through more complicated phase evolution from P’2-I→OP4→P’2-I→P’2-II. P’2-I to P’2-II phase transition in the process of deep sodiation is one of the key reasons for the deterioration of the P’2-type NMO cathode material's cyclic stability. As the formation of a new P’2-II phase must overcome additional kinetic barriers, unfavorable mutual stresses often occur at the interface of the two phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0048_R_d65546492e1334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, the uniform P’2-I phase of B-NMO during deep sodiation is conducive to the stability of the layered structure and the realization of long-term cycling. To confirm the detrimental effect of P’2-I to P’2-II phase transition during deep sodiation process, we adopted the voltage window of 1.5–3.5&nbsp;V and compared the cycling performance of NMO and B-NMO cathode materials in SIBs. Results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information) show that the capacity of NMO and B-NMO cathode materials was reduced due to the narrowed voltage window. The NMO cathode material showed rapidly capacity decay, while the B-NMO cathode material maintained its capacity during a long time cycling. Therefore, it can be concluded that the NMO cathode material performed worse in the deep sodiation process.</p>
            
            <p>Synchrotron X-ray PDF techniques can collect local structural information of atomic pairs by integrating the total scattering signals without limiting the crystallinity of the electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0028_R_d65546492e1345" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - NPG Asia Mater">28</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0049_R_d65546492e1345" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is suitable for tracking the structural changes in electrode materials under various voltages and exposing neglected local structural differences among various materials. In situ and ex situ synchrotron PDF characterization was utilized to identify the short-range structures of the NMO and B-NMO cathodes during desodiation and sodiation, and the results are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202306533-fig-0005">5</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0005">5a</a> indicates the in situ PDF stacking lines of NMO cathode, and peaks at different <i>r</i> (Å) are consistent to different atomic pairs. Firstly, peaks at ≈1.9 and 2.9&nbsp;Å are point to the in-plane nearest Mn–O and Mn–Mn pairs. Precisely, since the Jahn–Teller active Mn(III) leads to the distorted octahedron (MnO<sub>6</sub>) in the layered structure, there are four short Mn─O bonds (≈1.92&nbsp;Å) and two long Mn─O bonds (≈2.03&nbsp;Å) in the MnO<sub>6</sub> as drawn in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0005">5b</a>. Besides, there are also two nearly in-plane Mn–Mn distances (≈2.85 and 2.94&nbsp;Å) due to the Jahn–Teller distortion. The enlargement of the asymmetric peak of pristine NMO in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information) also confirms the presence of a distorted octahedron. Second, the peaks at ≈4.5, 5, and 6.4&nbsp;Å are associated with interlayer Mn–O/Mn–Mn distances. During the desodiation process, the intensity of the first and second peaks increased, and their symmetry also improved, showing decreased Jahn–Teller distortion characteristics, which were ascribed to the oxidation of Mn(III) to Mn(IV). The refinement results of the PDF pattern at 4.26&nbsp;V are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0005">5c</a>, which can be refined using <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> (P2) and <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mc</i> (O2) models. More precisely, the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra exhibit a high degree of symmetry with sixfold Mn─O bonds (≈1.92 Å), indicating minimal distortion of the octahedral structure. Moreover, the peaks at 6.4&nbsp;Å moved to 6&nbsp;Å at high voltages, indicating the contraction of <i>c</i>-axis, which is consistent with the phase transition from P’2-I to OP4 in the XRD measurements.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202306533-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6999de4f-31af-488f-a79b-ce9a97029e2a/adma202306533-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6999de4f-31af-488f-a79b-ce9a97029e2a/adma202306533-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/14adea23-d0c1-4888-80c3-c37a96c5a312/adma202306533-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6999de4f-31af-488f-a79b-ce9a97029e2a/adma202306533-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202306533-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202306533" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ synchrotron PDF patterns of a) NMO. b) Illustration of Jahn–Teller distortion for NMO. c) Refinement results of in situ PDF patterns for the charged and discharged NMO products. d) Refinement results of ex situ PDF patterns of the charged and discharged B-NMO products. e) Schematic of local structure changes during deep sodiation process of NMO and B-NMO cathodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Conversely, the interlayer peak is shifted back to ≈6.4&nbsp;Å at the beginning of discharge and finally moves to low <i>r</i> with the consecutive insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Above all, the peaks of in-plane Mn–O and Mn–Mn show arresting changes during later discharging: the significant asymmetry of Mn–O and the splitting of Mn–Mn peaks represent severe structural distortions after deep sodiation, which corresponds to the newly formed P’2-II phase. To investigate the NMO structure at the end of the discharge process, various crystal structures were employed to refine the discharged PDF pattern at 1.53&nbsp;V. The optimal outcome was achieved through a combination of <i>Cmcm</i> (P’2) and <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> (<i>α</i>-NaMnO<sub>2</sub>) models. For the <i>α</i>-NaMnO<sub>2</sub>, which is mentioned for the first time in the paper, it exhibits a monoclinic phase with a larger <i>β</i> angle (≈123°) and lower symmetry compared to the orthorhombic P’2 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0045_R_d65546492e1437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Crystals">45</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0046_R_d65546492e1437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Mater. Chem. A">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Because all Na<sup>+</sup> ions are located at octahedral sites, this material is also classified as having an O3-type structure. Therefore, the deep-sodiated product of NMO materials (labeled as the P’2-II phase) also shows an intergrowth structure with P- and O-type stacking of sodium-ion layers. As a consequence, the deep sodiated materials display more pronounced distortion, with their MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra featuring Mn─O bonds in 1.76 and 2.39&nbsp;Å. Subsequently, the spacing between adjacent Mn atoms has increased to as long as 3.11&nbsp;Å along the <i>ab</i> plane. The local distortion of deep-sodiated materials can be clearly identified and revealed through PDF characterization. The layered P’2-II materials exhibited a mixed structure of P- and O-type stacking in the sodium ion layers. Unlike the OP4 phase observed at high voltages, P’2-II phase materials have severely distorted MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, which typically generate anisotropic strain and disrupt the pristine structure. Therefore, preventing the phase transition during the discharge process is beneficial for stabilizing the cathode structures and enhancing their electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <p>Ex situ synchrotron PDF patterns in charged and discharged states and the corresponding refinement results for the B-NMO electrodes are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0005">5d</a>. Our results indicate that there are no splitting peaks for the B-NMO cathode materials after deep sodiation, and the pattern can be well refined using the single <i>Cmcm</i> model, which proves the good reversibility of the B-NMO materials. Mn─O bond lengths in the discharged B-NMO materials were ≈1.92 and 1.95&nbsp;Å, respectively, indicating a significantly reduced distortion compared to the discharged products of NMO. Short-range structural variations in the NMO and B-NMO materials during deep sodiation are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202306533-fig-0005">5e</a>. Unlike the unfavorable phase transition process of pristine NMO materials, the anchoring boron in the layered structure resulted in a turnaround. Owing to the appearance of B─O─Mn bonding, the stronger B–O covalent bonding (binding energy 809&nbsp;kJ&nbsp;mol<sup>–1</sup>) makes the oxygen atoms more negative,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0050_R_d65546492e1463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Electrochim. Acta">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thereby inhibiting the stretching of the Jahn–Teller active Mn(III) and finally maintaining the integrity and uniform change of the layered structure. Owing to the disappearance of the P’2-II phase, B-NMO materials exhibit less anisotropic strain and reversible structural evolution, which guarantee their sustainable cycling performance in SIBs. The results of the PDF analysis generally agree with those obtained by in situ XRD and provide additional information on the local structural changes in layered materials, thereby enhancing our understanding of their structural evolution.</p>
            
            <p>For a complete charge and discharge process, the first Mn(III) to Mn(IV) oxidation reaction during charging can alleviate the Jahn–Teller strain anisotropy and result in the formation of sixfold Mn─O bonds with reduced interlayer distances in both NMO and B-NMO. During sodiation, sodium ions are reinserted into the NMO-layered structure, causing a persistent reduction from Mn(IV) to Mn(III). This results in deformation of the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedron and stretching of the transition metal layers, ultimately leading to the accumulation of anisotropic strain within the interlayers. In addition, the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions resulted in greater electrostatic repulsion, and layer gilding occurred spontaneously to form a new P’2-II phase with decreased <i>c</i>-axis parameters. However, the introduction of boron ions into the layered interstitial sites can buffer the deformation of the transition metal layer caused by the reduction reaction of manganese ions, resulting in a gentle and continuous structural change, thereby prolonging the cycle life of the cathode material in SIBs. By combining XRD and PDF analyses, the structural evolution of NMO and B-NMO materials from both long- and short-range has been fundamentally disclosed. Compared with the XRD results, which mainly reveal the expansion or shrinkage of crystal parameters, the PDF characterization shows more intuitive evidence of MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedral distortion. Comparison of the degree of octahedral distortion revealed that phase transition during deep sodiation had significant adverse effects; thus, eliminating this phase transition plays a more essential role in improving the electrochemical performance. Moreover, the successful application of in situ PDF analysis to SIBs provides a new avenue for studying the correlation between their electrochemical behavior and detailed structural transformations.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202306533-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202306533-sec-0070-title">2.5 Thermal Stability and Bulk Degradation Studies</h3>
            
            <p>In previous extensively studied lithium-ion battery systems, charged high-voltage layered LiTMO<sub>2</sub> materials usually released O<sub>2</sub>/CO<sub>2</sub> gas, reacted with organic electrolytes, and caused fire and explosion hazards at high temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0003_R_d65546492e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Chem.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0051_R_d65546492e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, Na is highly reactive and flammable compared to Li; thus, the thermal safety of SIBs is questionable. The thermal safety properties of the layered NMO and B-NMO cathodes were examined by differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) analysis at elevated temperatures, which measured the enthalpy liberated for the cathode materials in the course of the temperature sweep.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0052_R_d65546492e1502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Power Sources">52</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0053_R_d65546492e1502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ex situ DSC spectra of the pristine and charged materials are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). For pristine materials, the DSC curve of B-NMO unveils one exothermic peak at a higher temperature (195&nbsp;°C) than that of NMO at 163&nbsp;°C. The different thermal behaviors result from the inserted boron ions inserted into the tetrahedral sites, and the stronger covalent B─O bonds further increase the energy of the layered structure, which resists thermal runaway at higher temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0026_R_d65546492e1510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0054_R_d65546492e1510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">54</a>, <a href="#adma202306533-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0055_R_d65546492e1510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Mater. Sci. Eng. B">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For electrochemically desodiated NMO and B-NMO, the decreased exothermic peak temperatures (144&nbsp;°C for NMO, 164&nbsp;°C for B-NMO) are associated with a lower Na<sup>+</sup> ion content and harmful side reactions between the electrode and electrolyte at 4.3&nbsp;V. In contrast, the B-NMO electrode is more stable than the NMO electrode, which also benefits from the high binding energy between boron and lattice oxygen. In summary, anchoring boron in a layered structure with stronger B─O covalent bonds can improve the thermal stability of layered materials in SIBs.</p>
            
            <p>Postmortem chemical and structural characterizations of the electrode materials after long-term cycling were completed by ex situ XRD and SEM analyses. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information) shows the XRD patterns of the NMO and B-NMO electrode materials after 100 cycles. The reduced peak relative intensity of NMO at 30–50° indicates a high degree of structural disorder after repeated (de)sodiation, which is caused by the inability to recover the original layered sequences and atomic locations after complicated phase transitions. Furthermore, a new hydration phase (Birnessite, JCPDS No. 43-1456) appeared in the cycled XRD patterns of the NMO materials. The formation of this phase is due to the intercalation of water molecules between the transition metal layers, as the larger transition metal layer distances and lower sodium ion content facilitate water molecule insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202306533-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202306533-bib-0056_R_d65546492e1523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, its appearance further proves that the layered structures of NMO materials are significantly destroyed after cycling. Comparatively, the cycled B-NMO cathode materials maintained an ordered crystal structure with a small hydration phase, whose sturdy layered structure is beneficial for the long-cycle performance of SIBs. Finally, the SEM images showed the structural integrity of the electrode materials after cycling, and the results are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). Ubiquitous cracks appeared in the cycled NMO cathode materials, proving the poor structural stability of the unmodified materials. During the deep sodiation process, several Na<sup>+</sup> ions are inserted into the layered structure via a reduction reaction from Mn(IV) to Mn(III) ions, which inevitably causes lattice distortion, volume shrinkage, and subsequent internal stress accumulation, accompanied by a phase transition from P’2-I to P’2-II. Here, the internal stress accumulated after repeated charging and discharging eventually led to the formation of intragranular microcracks, leading to the deterioration of the bulk structure and decay in its electrochemical capacity. These cracks in the particles cause detrimental effects, such as poor electronic/ionic conduction, transition metal dissolution, and side reactions with the electrolytes during cycling. In contrast, the B-NMO cathode materials retained their intact layered morphology after cycling, which is consistent with their better cycle stability in SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202306533-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202306533-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we demonstrated that the boron-anchoring strategy, involving the suppression of phase transition by easing the anisotropic strains and reducing the interlayer gliding, is feasible and valid for layered manganese-rich oxides. Systematic characterization and detailed structural analysis revealed that deep sodiation caused the transfer of pristine orthorhombic NMO cathode materials to the P’2-II phase with intergrowth P- and O-type transition metal layers stacking, which are one of the critical issues that lead to its structural deterioration and electrochemical performance degradation. Insertion of boron ions into tetrahedral sites successfully inhibited the P’2-I to P’2-II phase transition as well as dramatic lattice distortion at low voltages. The B-NMO electrode exhibited a remarkable capacity of 141&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 1&nbsp;C and drastically enhanced cycling stability with a capacity retention of 81% after 100 cycles between 1.5 and 4.3&nbsp;V. Moreover, the layered structure of B-NMO materials was intact after long-term cycling without any intragranular cracks. This study reveals the adverse effects of deep sodiation on layered Mn-rich cathode materials and provides insights for the sustainable development of advanced cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
